<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Chromosome-level genome assembly of a parent species of widely cultivated azaleas"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-10-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Azaleas (Ericaceae) comprise one of the most diverse ornamental plants, renowned for their cultural and economic importance. We present a chromosome-scale genome assembly for Rhododendron simsii, the primary ancestor of azalea cultivars. Genome analyses unveil the remnants of an ancient whole-genome duplication preceding the radiation of most Ericaceae, likely contributing to the genomic architecture of flowering time. Small-scale gene duplications contribute to the expansion of gene families involved in azalea pigment biosynthesis. We reconstruct entire metabolic pathways for anthocyanins and carotenoids and their potential regulatory networks by detailed analysis of time-ordered gene co-expression networks. MYB, bHLH, and WD40 transcription factors may collectively regulate anthocyanin accumulation in R. simsii, particularly at the initial stages of flower coloration, and with WRKY transcription factors controlling progressive flower coloring at later stages. This work provides a cornerstone for understanding the underlying genetics governing flower timing and coloration and could accelerate selective breeding in azalea. Azaleas are one of the most diverse ornamental plants and have cultural and economic importance. Here, the authors report a chromosome-scale genome assembly for the primary ancestor of the azalea cultivar Rhododendro simsi and identify transcription factors that may function in flower coloration at different stages."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Azaleas (Ericaceae) comprise one of the most diverse ornamental plants, renowned for their cultural and economic importance. We present a chromosome-scale genome assembly for <i>Rhododendron simsii</i>, the primary ancestor of azalea cultivars. Genome analyses unveil the remnants of an ancient whole-genome duplication preceding the radiation of most Ericaceae, likely contributing to the genomic architecture of flowering time. Small-scale gene duplications contribute to the expansion of gene families involved in azalea pigment biosynthesis. We reconstruct entire metabolic pathways for anthocyanins and carotenoids and their potential regulatory networks by detailed analysis of time-ordered gene co-expression networks. MYB, bHLH, and WD40 transcription factors may collectively regulate anthocyanin accumulation in <i>R. simsii</i>, particularly at the initial stages of flower coloration, and with WRKY transcription factors controlling progressive flower coloring at later stages. This work provides a cornerstone for understanding the underlying genetics governing flower timing and coloration and could accelerate selective breeding in azalea.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Rhododendron</i> (Ericaceae) harbors more than 1000 species and 30,000 cultivars and is known for the outstanding beauty and diversity of its corollas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Geng, Y. Y. The genus Rhododendron of China (Shanghai Scientific and Technical Publishers, Shanghai, 2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64132e809">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Sleumer, H. Ein system der gattung Rhododendron L. Botanische Jahrb. Syst. 74, 511–553 (1949)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64132e812">2</a></sup>. During the 18th century, several Chinese azalea cultivars were introduced to Europe (especially to England and Belgium), facilitating their breeding for ornamental use<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e816">3</a></sup>. <i>R. simsii</i> (potted azalea) is an evergreen species of the subgenus <i>Tsutsusi</i>, endemic to East Asia, and is the most widely cultivated <i>Rhododendron</i> species with numerous cultivars selected from a diversity of wild relatives and natural hybrids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e830">3</a></sup>. At present, the annual production of <i>R. simsii</i> hybrids has reached approximately 40 million pots in Belgium alone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e837">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tanaka, Y., Sasaki, N. &amp; Ohmiya, A. Biosynthesis of plant pigments: anthocyanins, betalains and carotenoids. Plant J. 54, 733–749 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d64132e840">4</a></sup>. Although <i>R. simsii</i> natural blooming extends from the end of spring to the beginning of summer, some ‘easy-care and color-rich’ cultivars can bloom as early as Christmas and Spring Festivals, when few other flowering plants are available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Sleumer, H. Ein system der gattung Rhododendron L. Botanische Jahrb. Syst. 74, 511–553 (1949)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64132e847">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e850">3</a></sup>. Currently, azalea cultivars have become the focus of intensive ornamental use through hybridizations and production programs, attaining prominence as pot plants and landscape shrubs in Europe, North American, and Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e855">3</a></sup>.</p><p>Breeding and development of new ornamental cultivars is mainly focused on flower color. Moreover, flower color is also of paramount importance for plant ecology and evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wessinger, C. A. &amp; Rausher, M. D. Lessons from flower colour evolution on targets of selection. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 5741–5749 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d64132e862">5</a></sup>. Since the work of Gregor Mendel (1856–1863), flower color has successfully contributed to the elucidation of the fundamental principles of genetics, while more recently, genomics has provided insights into the evolution of the biochemical pathways and regulatory networks underlying phenotypic traits, including flower color<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wessinger, C. A. &amp; Rausher, M. D. Lessons from flower colour evolution on targets of selection. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 5741–5749 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d64132e866">5</a></sup>. So far, pigment analyses for azalea flowers were based on pigment type and composition percentage; however, their underlying genetic and regulatory mechanisms are largely unknown. Recently, some of the flavonoid biosynthesis structural genes were isolated from several <i>Rhododendron</i> species and cultivars, and the spatiotemporal expression patterns of some key node-genes were analyzed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e873">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tanaka, Y., Sasaki, N. &amp; Ohmiya, A. Biosynthesis of plant pigments: anthocyanins, betalains and carotenoids. Plant J. 54, 733–749 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d64132e876">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mizuta, D., Ban, T., Miyajima, I., Nakatsuka, A. &amp; Kobayashi, N. Comparison of flower color with anthocyanin composition patterns in evergreen azalea. Sci. Horticult. 122, 594–602 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64132e879">6</a></sup>. Although two <i>Rhododendron</i> genomes were released recently, both were obtained by Illumina short read sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Soza, V. L. et al. The Rhododendron genome and chromosomal organization provide insight into shared whole genome duplications across the heath family (Ericaceae). Genome Biol. Evol. 11, 3357–3371 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d64132e887">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhang, L. et al. The draft genome assembly of Rhododendron delavayi Franch. var. delavayi. Gigascience 6, 1–11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64132e890">8</a></sup>. The lack of a high-quality <i>Rhododendron</i> species whole-genome sequence has seriously hampered the unraveling of their color formation, in spite of the known long-term breeding history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e897">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhang, L. et al. The draft genome assembly of Rhododendron delavayi Franch. var. delavayi. Gigascience 6, 1–11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64132e900">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we present a chromosome-scale genome assembly for <i>R. simsii</i>. The genome of <i>R. simsii</i> is determined by a combination of long-read sequencing and Hi-C scaffolding technologies. In total, a 529 Mb genome sequence is assembled, and &gt;91% of the genome could be anchored on 13 chromosomes with a scaffold N50 of 36 Mb. We detect the remnants of a whole genome duplication and find tandem and other small-scale gene duplications to be the key drivers for gene family expansions. Furthermore, we unravel the metabolic co-expression network of flower pigmentation and identified the structural genes, and their potential regulators, of flower coloring through time-ordered comparative transcriptome analyses. The availability of this reference genome sequence and information on the molecular basis and the genetic mechanisms governing flower color in <i>Rhododendron</i> present valuable resources for the development of consumer-oriented selective breeding novelties of azalea.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3"><i>R. simsii</i> genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The genome size of <i>R. simsii</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">1</a>) estimated with <i>K</i>-mer analysis to be ~525 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">2</a>) was larger than its size estimation by flow cytometry for yet unresolved reasons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">1</a>). We produced 100× coverage of PacBio long-read sequencing data, 170× coverage of short reads of PCR-free Illumina sequencing, and 100× coverage of Hi-C paired-end reads (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">3</a>). After primary assembly, comparison, correction, polishing, and scaffolding, a final assembly of 529 Mb was obtained. The assembly is slightly larger than the estimated genome size, which may be due to high heterozygosity (~1.78%, estimated with <i>K</i>-mer frequency, see Supplementary Note 1 for details). After mapping the Illumina reads to the final assembly, single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) were identified with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d64132e956">9</a></sup> (with default settings) and obtained a SNP heterozygosity level of ~1.07% and a single base error rate of ~0.0054% was obtained. There was no obvious GC bias in the sequencing data from PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) technology; however, a GC bias was detected for the Illumina sequencing data <b>(</b>Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">4</a><b>)</b>, confirming the advantage of PacBio SMRT technology over Illumina for genome sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ross, M. G. et al. Characterizing and measuring bias in sequence data. Genome Biol. 14, R51 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d64132e969">10</a></sup>, in addition to the longer read length provided.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 The statistics for genome sequencing of <i>Rhododendron</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The final assembly consisted of 911 contigs and 552 scaffolds (13 chromosome-level scaffolds, one chloroplast genome, one mitochondrial genome, and 537 super contigs) with contig N50 of 2.2 Mb and scaffold N50 of 36 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">5</a>). The number of chromosome-scale super scaffolds is consistent with the species’ determined chromosome number of 13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chen, R. Chromosome atlas of major economic plants genome in China. (Science Press, Beijing, 2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1716">11</a></sup>. The high fidelity of the assembly was supported by the high 10-fold minimum genome coverage of 99.3% (Illumina) and 98.6% (PacBio), and the high mapping rates of 93.3% (Illumina) and 90.9% (PacBio). The high completeness of this assembly was also evidenced by a 93.7% (1349 genes) Benchmarking Universal Single Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) recovery score<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1721">12</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">4</a>), which is better compared to two recently released <i>Rhododendron</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Soza, V. L. et al. The Rhododendron genome and chromosomal organization provide insight into shared whole genome duplications across the heath family (Ericaceae). Genome Biol. Evol. 11, 3357–3371 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1734">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhang, L. et al. The draft genome assembly of Rhododendron delavayi Franch. var. delavayi. Gigascience 6, 1–11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1737">8</a></sup> obtained by Illumina short read sequencing (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">6</a>). Judged by the high long terminal repeat (LTR) Assembly Index (LAI)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res. 46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1745">13</a></sup> score of 18.10, the <i>R. simsii</i> genome attained reference level quality.</p><p>We predicted 34,170 genes, and the average lengths for total gene regions, transcript, coding sequence (CDS), exon sequence, and intron sequence are 5089.2, 1416.3, 1288.7, 259.7, and 403.1 bp, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">5</a>). A total of 32,999 protein-coding genes were predicted, which is considerably more than those annotated for the <i>R. williamsianum</i> genome (23,559 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Soza, V. L. et al. The Rhododendron genome and chromosomal organization provide insight into shared whole genome duplications across the heath family (Ericaceae). Genome Biol. Evol. 11, 3357–3371 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1764">7</a></sup>, but similar to those for the <i>R. delavayi</i> genome (32,938)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhang, L. et al. The draft genome assembly of Rhododendron delavayi Franch. var. delavayi. Gigascience 6, 1–11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1772">8</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a>). In addition, we predicted 482 tRNAs, 64 rRNAs including eight 28S, six 18S and 50 5S rRNAs, and 625 other non-coding RNAs (211 miRNAs, 16 tRNAs and 158 snoRNAs) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>Among the predicted protein-coding genes, 96.44% could be annotated through at least one of the following protein-related databases: the NCBI non-redundant protein database (NR) (85.70%), the Swiss-Prot protein database (57.80%), the Translated European Molecular Biology Laboratory (TrEMBL) database (84.90%), the protein families database (Pfam) (73.60%), and the Gene Ontology (GO) database (57.54%) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4"><i>R. simsii</i> is an ancient polyploid</h3><p>A phylogenetic tree was constructed for the 15 species of asterids and two outgroup species, using 806 orthogroups (see “Methods” section). The phylogenetic relationship between and within the main clades (Asterid I, Asterid II, and Ericales) agree with previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1805">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657–666 (2016)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1805_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1805_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1808">17</a></sup>. Molecular dating suggests that <i>R. simsii</i> diverged from the most recent common ancestor of <i>R. delavayi</i> and <i>R. williamsianum</i> around 14.54 Mya, following the divergence of <i>Rhododendron</i> and <i>Vaccinium corymbosum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Colle, M. et al. Haplotype-phased genome and evolution of phytonutrient pathways of tetraploid blueberry. Gigascience 8, giz012 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1827">18</a></sup> around 55.93 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome evolutionary history."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genome evolutionary history.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="726" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Chronogram showing divergence times and genome duplications in asterids (asterid II, asterid I, Ericales, and Cornales), with node age and the 95% confidence intervals labeled. Resolved polyploidization events are shown with blue (duplications) and red translucent dots (triplications). Pie charts show the proportions of gene families that underwent expansion or contraction. Predicated WGD only shown for Ericales and Cornales. *WGD identified in this study. <sup>#</sup>WGD reported in Wang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Wang, J. P. et al. Two likely auto-tetraploidization events shaped kiwifruit genome and contributed to establishment of the Actinidiaceae family. iScience 7, 230–240 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1850">69</a></sup>. <sup>%</sup>WGD reported in Colle et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Colle, M. et al. Haplotype-phased genome and evolution of phytonutrient pathways of tetraploid blueberry. Gigascience 8, giz012 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1856">18</a></sup>. <b>b</b> <i>K</i>s distribution on the upper right (insert) is showing <i>K</i>s distribution from orthologs between <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and each of the five species (<i>Rhododendron delavayi</i>, <i>R. simsii</i>, <i>R. williamsianum</i>, <i>Camellia sinensis</i>, and <i>Camptotheca acuminata</i>). <i>K</i>s distribution in the lower left showing <i>K</i>s distribution from paralogs within <i>Rhododendron delavayi</i>, <i>R. simsii</i>, <i>R. williamsianum</i>, <i>Camellia sinensis</i>, and <i>Camptotheca acuminata</i>. <b>c</b> Synteny and distribution of genomic features. a, the density of Ogre, a family of <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RT. b, the density of Tekay, a family of <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RT. <b>c</b>, The density of all <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RT. <b>d</b>, the density of all <i>Copia</i> LTR-RT. <b>e</b>, gene density. <b>f</b>, histogram of GC content. A map connecting homologous regions of the genome is shown. The red lines represent syntenic regions for the WGD event (78 Mya) and blue lines represent γ-WGD event. The line segments of bold gray in outer circles indicate predicated centromeres and pericentromeric regions on the 13 chromosomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Synonymous substitutions per site (<i>K</i>s) age distributions and collinearity/synteny analyses unveiled evidence for an ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in the lineage leading to <i>Rhododendron</i>, around 78 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1a</a>) (see “Methods” section for details), with three <i>Rhododendron</i> species (<i>R. simsii</i>, <i>R. delavayi</i>, and <i>R. williamsianum</i>) showing signature <i>K</i>s peaks at about 0.65 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1b</a> lower left). Moreover, intra-genomic analysis showed that 18.83% of the genome lies within duplicated segments (6,213/32,999 collinear genes distributed along 289 duplicated blocks; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">8</a>). We also built a gene homology dot plot from one randomly selected set of chromosomes from the tetraploid <i>V. corymbosum</i> and the 13 chromosome-level scaffolds of <i>R. simsii</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">7</a>). Homologous regions showed a 1:1 correspondence, suggesting that azalea did not share the recent WGD event reported for blueberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Colle, M. et al. Haplotype-phased genome and evolution of phytonutrient pathways of tetraploid blueberry. Gigascience 8, giz012 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d64132e1991">18</a></sup>. At the same time, we detected a 2:1 syntenic relationship between <i>R. simsii</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2000">14</a></sup>, a 4:2 syntenic relationship between <i>Actinidia chinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Pilkington, S. M. et al. A manually annotated Actinidia chinensis var. chinensis (kiwifruit) genome highlights the challenges associated with draft genomes and gene prediction in plants. BMC Genomics 19, 257 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2007">19</a></sup> and <i>R. simsii</i>, and a 4:1 syntenic relationship between <i>V. corymbosum</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>, which provide additional evidence for a WGD event in the common ancestor of <i>Rhohodendron</i>, <i>Vaccinium</i>, and <i>Actinidia</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">7</a>). Apart from the WGDs described above, <i>Ks</i> distributions also provide evidence for WGDs in <i>Camellia sinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2042">20</a></sup> (<i>K</i>s peak at 0.45 and duplication time around 68 Mya) and <i>Camptotheca acuminata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhao, D. et al. De novo genome assembly of Camptotheca acuminata, a natural source of the anti-cancer compound camptothecin. Gigascience 6, 1–7 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2051">21</a></sup> (0.4 and 62 Mya, respectively) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1b</a> lower left).</p><p>We also calculated <i>K</i>s values of one-versus-one orthologs between <i>V. vinifera</i> and five asterids species (<i>R. simsii</i>, <i>R. delavayi</i>, <i>R. williamsianum, C. sinensis</i>, and <i>C. acuminata</i>) and calculated the number of substitutions per synonymous site per year (<i>r</i>) with the formula, following <i>r</i> = <i>K</i>s/(2 × (divergence time))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2089">16</a></sup>. From this relative rate test, we found that species from Ericales and Cornales have a similar substitution rate (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1b</a> upper right).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5"><i>Gypsy</i> dominated pericentromeric regions in <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>We predicted 954,329 repeat elements, totaling 250,988,768 bp (47.48%) sequence of the assembled genome, containing predominantly known transposable elements (TEs) (25.56%), uncharacterized TEs (19.24%), and a smaller proportion of simple repeats (1.41%). Repeat annotations are provided in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">9</a>. The uncharacterized (unknown) TEs may contain highly degenerated TE copies or lack distinct protein-coding sequences required for further classification. More work is needed to elucidate the structural and/or sequence diversity of the uncharacterized TEs.</p><p>Long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) represented the highest portion (17.01%) in the genome, with superfamilies of <i>Gypsy</i> (11.90% of the genome sequence) and <i>Copia</i> (4.00%) being dominant. However, we found that <i>R. simsii</i> features low LTR-RT accumulation (<i>S</i> + <i>T</i> + <i>I</i> = 14,577) but high removal rates (proportion of LTR clusters with <i>S</i>:<i>I</i> &gt; 3), where <i>S</i> is solo-LTR, <i>T</i> is truncated LTR-RT and <i>I</i> is intact LTR-RT (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">10</a>), which may be one reason for the overall lower proportion of TE in <i>R. simsii</i> than in other species analyzed here.</p><p>A total of 825 intact <i>Gypsy</i> (8.68 Mb) and 1303 intact <i>Copia</i> (8.20 Mb) LTR-TRs were identified across the whole <i>R. simsii</i> genome, with recent bursts, as well as a single ancient amplification peak predicted at ~2 Mya for most clades of both <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">11</a>). Overall, we found 10.5-84.1% among the different subgroups of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> preferentially residing in gene regions, particularly the chloroplast RNA splicing and ribosome maturation (CRM) family of <i>Gypsy</i> (57.63%) and the Ale family of <i>Copia</i> (52.73%), Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">10a, b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">11</a>. However, most members of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> were found 3-5 Kb distant from genic regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">10c</a>). Insertion dynamics were similar between LTR-TRs proximal to and far away from genes, and among subgroups of both <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">10d, e and f</a>).</p><p>Gene density decreased and GC content increased from the chromosome ends towards the centromeres (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">11</a>). We found that the accumulated <i>Gypsy</i> elements tend to be clustered in the pericentromeric regions, particularly Tekay and Ogre, yet there was a relatively even distribution for <i>Copia</i> elements along the chromosomes. Moreover, it was found that more <i>Copia</i> elements experienced positive selection (<i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s &gt; 1) in their reverse transcriptase (RT) domains, while RT domains of <i>Gypsy</i> elements showed lower <i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s ratios and relatively smaller <i>K</i>a values (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">12</a>). The distinct behaviors of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> remain to be elucidated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">TD/PD contributed to gene family expansion in <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>We identified 29,396 duplicated genes that were classified into five different categories: 6056 whole-genome duplicates (WGD duplicates, 18.4%), 4746 tandem duplicates (TD, 14.4%), 3732 proximal duplicates (PD, 11.3%), 6399 transposed duplicates (TRD, 19.4%), and 8463 dispersed duplicates (DSD, 25.6%) (see “Methods” section, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">13</a>). We compared the <i>K</i>s and <i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s distribution among different modes of gene duplication. Higher <i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s ratios and smaller <i>K</i>s values were found for tandem and proximal duplicate gene pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">14</a>), suggesting an ongoing and continuous process for tandem and proximal duplications and more rapid sequence divergence and stronger positive selection than genes originated through other duplication modes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Gene duplication and evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Gene duplication and evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="709" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> The <i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s ratio distributions of gene pairs derived from different modes of duplication. WGD whole-genome duplication, TD tandem duplication, PD proximal duplication, TRD transposed duplication, DSD dispersed duplication. In the boxplot representations, points are outliers; the center line is the median; the lower and upper hinges correspond to the first and third quartiles (25th and 75th percentiles); whiskers extend to the minimum (left whiskers) and maximum (right whiskers) estimates located within the 1.5× interquartile range (IQR) of the first and third quartiles, respectively. Gaussian kernel estimates of <i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s of different duplicated groups are shown as violins; 6056 WGD, 4746 TD, 3732 PD, 6399 TRD, and 8463 DSD duplicates were incorporated in the statistics. <b>b</b> Venn diagram shows the possible logical relations between members of expanded gene family and modes of duplication (WGD, TD, PD, TRD, and DSD). TDp: tandem duplication experienced positive selection, PDp: proximal duplication experienced positive selection, DSDp: dispersed duplication experienced positive selection, EGFs: expanded gene families. <b>c</b> Functional enrichment analysis of genes overlapping between expanded gene families (EGFs) and five modes of duplication (WGD, TD, PD, TRD, and DSD). The enriched GO terms with corrected <i>P</i> value &lt;0.001 are presented. The color of circles represents the statistical significance of enriched GO terms. The size of the circles represents the number of genes in a GO term. For all annotated genes their GO terms are provided as background information. ‘<i>P</i> adjust’ is the Benjamini–Hochberg false discovery rate (FDR) adjusted <i>P</i> value. <b>d</b> The numbers of genes from different families and gene duplications are provided. SL singletons.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>All predicted gene models for the 17 species were clustered using OrthoFinder (version 2.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2369">22</a></sup>, resulting in 22,455 orthogroups (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">13</a>). Using CAFÉ (version 4.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2379">23</a></sup>, 1515 gene families (6,754 genes) were found to be expanded, while 1657 gene families were found to be contracted in <i>R. simsii</i>, with 57.6% of the expanded gene families (EGFs) due to TD and PD duplications (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig2">2b</a>). Hypergeometric tests were performed for overlapping genes between expanded gene families and WGD-TD-PD-TRD-DSD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig2">2c</a>). TD-EGFs and PD-EGFs genes were found exhibiting divergent enriched GO terms. For example, TD-EGFs enriched categories are implicated in plant self-defense, development and adaptation, while PD-EGFs genes are enriched in GO terms involved in ‘binding’ and ‘recognition’. In brief, newly generated tandem and proximal duplications have been important sources of gene family expansion in <i>R. simsii</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><p>To verify whether the identified tandem gene clusters are real and not artificial due to errors in the genome assembly, we mapped our PacBio long-reads back to the assembly, and examined whether the pair of adjacent tandem duplicated genes or the intergenic region could be fully or partially recovered by the mapped long-reads. We indeed found that most (79–87%) of the duplicated genes could be recovered, fully or partially, both for transcription factor (TF) or anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthetic genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">15</a>). These results provide very good evidence for the true existence of tandem gene clusters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Flowering-time genes in <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>We detected 424 genes related to flowering-time control in <i>R. simsii</i> by querying the Flowering Interactive Database, FLOR-ID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bouche, F., Lobet, G., Tocquin, P. &amp; Perilleux, C. FLOR-ID: an interactive database of flowering-time gene networks in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1167–D1171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2421">24</a></sup>, in which 295 flowering-time genes are functionally characterized for <i>Arabidopsis</i>. With regards to gene function, the categories ‘General’ and ‘Photoperiodism, light perception and signaling’ were represented in large proportions of 37.26% (158) and 20.99% (89) among all flowering-time control genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig2">2d</a>). There was a clear time-gradient of expression of the flowering-time related genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">16</a>) across the five examined flower coloring stages. In addition, we identified a gene family (OG0000614) encoding 13 members of high-affinity sucrose transporters that may play key roles in flowering transition delay<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Sivitz, A. B. et al. Arabidopsis sucrose transporter AtSUC9. High-affinity transport activity, intragenic control of expression, and early flowering mutant phenotype. Plant Physiol. 143, 188–198 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2435">25</a></sup>. This gene family was identified by CAFÉ as being expanded in azalea, and its members showed continuous upregulation in the corolla during the flowering time-series (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Flower pigmentation genes in <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>Our high-quality genome assembly allowed reconstruction of the metabolic pathway for flower coloration, specifically capturing the implicated enzymatic genes in this process. We unveiled 58 genes encoding enzymes functioning in the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">17</a>) and 125 enzymatic genes with predicted functions in anthocyanin and flavonol biosynthesis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">18</a>). Flavonoids are synthesized by a branched pathway that yields both colored anthocyanin pigments and colorless flavonols. Furthermore, we predicted five genes encoding three enzymes of blue-anthocyanins modification, including one anthocyanin 5-(6”‘-hydroxycinnamoyltransferase) (5AT|3AT, one of gentiodelphin-acyltransferase, EC 2.3.1.153), two ternatin C3-acyltransferases (ternatin C3-AT, EC 3.2.2.24), and two viodelphin-glucosyltransferases (viodelphin-GT, EC 2.3.1.-) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The biosynthesis pathways of two kinds of flavonoids, anthocyanin and flavonol."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The biosynthesis pathways of two kinds of flavonoids, anthocyanin and flavonol.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="484" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genes identified in tandem clusters are displayed as blue while proximal cluster genes are displayed as brown. We divided the flavonoid enzymes encoding genes into two groups designated as ‘early’ and ‘late’, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Pelletier, M. K., Murrell, J. R. &amp; Shirley, B. W. Characterization of flavonol synthase and leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase genes in Arabidopsis (further evidence for differential regulation of “early” and “late” genes). Plant Physiol. 113, 1437–1445 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2482">70</a></sup>. The ‘early’ biosynthesis genes (EBGs) encode enzymes functioning more upstream towards the entrance into the pathway, whereas the ‘late’ biosynthesis genes (LBGs) exhibit highly distinct expression patterns. PAL phenylalanine ammonia-lyase (EC 4.3.1.24), C4H: cinnamate-4-hydroxylase (EC 1.14.14.91), 4CL 4-coumarate CoA ligase 4 (EC 6.2.1.12), CHS chalcone synthase (EC 2.3.1.74), CHI chalcone isomerase (EC 5.5.1.6), F3H flavanone 3-hydroxylase (EC 1.14.11.9), F3′H flavonoid 3′-hydroxylase (EC 1.14.13.21), F3′5′H flavonoid 3′,5′-hydroxylase (EC 1.14.13.88), DFR dihydroflavonol 4-reductase (EC 1.1.1.219), ANS anthocyanidin synthase (EC 1.14.11.19), F3oGT flavonol-3-O-glucosyl transferase (EC 2.4.1.115), AT acyltransferase, GT glucosyltransferase, FLS flavonol synthase (EC 1.14.11.23). Gene expression profile (in normalized TPMs) in different time points of flowering (here T1-T5, from left to right in each heatmap panel) are presented in the heatmap alongside the gene names. The bar represents the expression level of each gene (<i>z</i>-score). Low to high expression is indicated by a change in color from blue to red.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene duplication events linked to flowering time and color</h3><p>TD genes were found to be enriched for GO terms related to secondary metabolic biosynthetic and modification processes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig2">2c</a>). Furthermore, the proportion of TD/PD genes linked to flower coloration was significantly higher in <i>R. simsii</i> than that in related species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">14</a>). Counting the number of enzymatic genes in tandem or proximal gene clusters unveiled that TD/PD duplications substantially contributed to the observed proportions of enzymatic genes for the anthocyanin/flavonol versus the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway. TD/PD duplications affected 17% of genes for carotenoid biosynthesis in the <i>R. simsii</i> genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">17</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">15</a>) and 42% of the genes for anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">15</a>), respectively. In addition, important TD/PD events were also found for 52.63% (10/19) of the genes coding for 4CL (4-coumarate: CoA ligase 4; EC 6.2.1.12), 57.14% (8/14) encoding CHS (chalcone synthase; EC 2.3.1.74), 60% (3/5) encoding DFR (dihydroflavonol 4-reductase; EC 1.1.1.219), 48.48% (16/33) encoding F3H (flavanone 3-hydroxylase; EC 1.14.11.9), 35.29% (6/17) encoding FLS (Flavonol synthase; EC 1.14.11.23), 55.56% (10/18) encoding F3′H (flavonoid 3′-hydroxylase; EC 1.14.13.21), 58.33% (7/12) encoding F3′5′H (flavonoid 3′,5′-hydroxylase; EC 1.14.13.88) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig3">3</a>). The diversity of the expression patterns indicates that further studies are necessary to verify and characterize the functions of the TD/PD duplicates.</p><p>In contrast, TD and PD duplications seem to have accounted for much lower proportions of expansions of flowering-time genes (6.82% (30/424) and 6.84% 424), respectively)/424), respectively), while WGD appears to have contributed the largest proportion (159/424, 37.5%) of flowering-time control gene copies and more than other modes of gene duplication (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig2">2d</a>). Interestingly, genes involved in flowering-time control are mostly present as low-copy gene families, 101 genes from single-copy families, 128 genes from dual-copy families and 60 genes from triple-copy families (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">The dynamics of flower pigmentation regulation in <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>We performed time-ordered comparative transcriptome analyses (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig4">4a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">20</a>) and found 8067 genes (618 TFs and 7,449 structural genes) expressed with an average Transcripts Per Kilobase Million (TPM) greater than 0.5 and exhibiting significant differentiation between any two samples among the five flowering time stages (T1–T5). As the initial node, a basic helix-loop-helix (bHLH) transcription factor (<i>Rhsim13G0024200</i>), highly expressed at the very first time point but not later, was selected to generate a time-ordered gene co-expression network (TO-GCN)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chang, Y. M. et al. Comparative transcriptomics method to infer gene coexpression networks and its applications to maize and rice leaf transcriptomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 3091–3099 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2563">26</a></sup>. Eight time-series expression levels (L1-L8, nodes &gt;20) centering on TFs were finally reconstructed using the suggested<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chang, Y. M. et al. Comparative transcriptomics method to infer gene coexpression networks and its applications to maize and rice leaf transcriptomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 3091–3099 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2567">26</a></sup> positive/negative cutoff values (0.81; −0.57) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig4">4b</a>). TO-GCN revealed a co-expression network involving the 618 differentially expressed TFs and 62 genes from the carotenoid/anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis pathways (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig4">4b</a>). Time-course transcriptomes were further distinguished for the initial (T1, flower appears white and semi-transparent), the transitional (T2 and T3 for light red flower), and the terminal (T4 and T5 for the determined red flower color) stages of corolla pigmentation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig4">4c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Time-ordered gene co-expression network related to flower coloring."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Time-ordered gene co-expression network related to flower coloring.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="497" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Five flower developmental stages (T1-T5). <b>b</b> Predicted regulatory network and the connection among TFs and structural genes involved in carotenoid and anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis pathways. Inside the pink circles, purple nodes represent <i>MYB</i> genes, olive nodes represent <i>bHLH</i>, turquoise nodes represent <i>WD40</i> genes, wathet blue nodes represent <i>WRKY</i> genes, and green nodes represent <i>ERF</i> genes. Pink nodes represent remaining TFs. Outside pink circles, orange points represent the enzymatic genes of the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway, locating on the top, crimson points and green points on the bottom represent enzymatic genes involved in anthocyanin and flavonol biosynthesis. L1 to L8 indicate the levels identified in the f-ordered gene co-expression network. <b>c</b>, the heatmaps of average normalized TPMs (<i>z</i>-score) at each time point of flowering at each level identified in the time-ordered gene regulatory network. Three stages of flower coloring were identified as the initial (T1), transitional (T2–T3) and terminal (T4–T5) stages, based on the expression profile. The bar represents the expression level of each gene (<i>z</i>-score). Low to high expression is indicated by a change in color from blue to red.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The general pattern elucidated that most network members (358, including 328 TFs) appeared at the initial stage (T1), 177 genes (including 150 TFs) at the terminal stage (T4–T5) and 70 genes (65 TFs) at the transitional stage (T2–T3). Likewise, T1 (corresponding to time-series levels L1–L3) showed the highest TPM, followed by genes assigned to the transitional stage (T2–T3, corresponding to L4–L5), and finally the genes that were expressed at the terminal red color determining stage (T4–T5, corresponding to L6–L8). Functional enrichment analysis revealed that distinct gene functions are turned on and off, respectively, at these different stages (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">21</a>). Yet, some genes encoding key biosynthetic enzymes in color determination tend to be continuously upregulated, such as those in the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway encoding phytoene synthase (PSY; EC 2.5.1.32), carotene isomerase (CRTISO; EC 5.2.1.13) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">17</a>), and anthocyanidin synthase (ANS; EC 1.14.11.19) in the anthocyanin biosynthesis pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig3">3</a>).</p><p>More specifically, at the initial stage (T1), we found that 14 enzymatic genes of the carotenoid biosynthesis expressed at high levels (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">16</a>). Among the 194 TFs that appeared as potential direct regulators of these enzymatic genes, <i>WD40</i> family members were most numerous (21). For anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis, we detected 16 enzymatic genes expressed at high levels at T1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig5">5b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">16</a>), and 120 TFs as their potential direct regulators. For genes implicated in the sub-network (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig5">5c</a>) for anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis and their associated <i>bHLH</i>, <i>WD40</i>, and <i>MYB</i> TFs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">17</a>), a clear gene expression decline was evident (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">22</a>), exemplified for some of these TFs and their direct target, a dihydrokaempferol biosynthetic gene <i>F3H</i> (<i>Rhsim11G0126300</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig5">5b–d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Sub-network for different pigment biosynthesis pathways in the initial stage."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Sub-network for different pigment biosynthesis pathways in the initial stage.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc"
