/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Sub-network for carotenoid biosynthesis. <b>b</b> Sub-network for anthocyanin and flavonol biosynthesis. <b>c</b> Sub-network for anthocyanin and flavonol biosynthesis with <i>MYB</i>, <i>bHLH</i>, and <i>WD40</i> gene families. <b>d</b> Heatmaps of average normalized TPMs (<i>z</i>-score) at each time point of flowering. <i>F3H</i>, a gene encoding a key enzyme in the synthesis of dihydrokaempferol for flavonols/anthocyanins metabolic pathways, and 16 TFs (six <i>bHLH</i>, four <i>MYB</i>, and six <i>WD40</i>) identified as its direct regulators. The bar represents the expression level of each gene (<i>z</i>-score). Low to high expression is indicated by a change in color from blue to red. Edges between structural genes were not shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The terminal coloration stage (T4–T5) showed maximal expression for substantial numbers of TFs and pigment biosynthetic genes. For anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis, we predicted that seven enzymatic genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">16</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig6">6a</a>) expressed at high levels were directly regulated by 74 potential regulators, mostly ethylene-responsive element binding factors (<i>ERF</i>s) (19) and <i>WRKY</i> (16) family members. The phylogenies, conserved motifs and gene structures of these TFs associated with flower coloration were further examined (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">24</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Sub-network for different pigment biosynthesis pathways in the terminal stage."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Sub-network for different pigment biosynthesis pathways in the terminal stage.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18771-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18771_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="298" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Sub-network for anthocyanin and flavonol biosynthesis. <b>b</b> DNA binding sites detected in the 2 Kb upstream sequences of five core genes and the potential regulatory elements (here TFs). <b>c</b> Resolved hierarchical regulation for <i>F3H</i> gene. The bar represents the expression level of each gene (<i>z</i>-score). Low to high expression is indicated by a change in color from blue to red. Edges between structural genes were not shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>F3H represents an important rate-limited enzyme in anthocyanins/flavonols synthesis, but its upstream regulators have remained elusive. By examining the co-expression network inferred from TO-GCN (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig6">6b, c</a>), we identified the potential first-order to third-order upstream regulators. Here, we identified one <i>F3H</i> gene (<i>Rhsim03G0111400</i>) as the hub within the potential regulatory network relationships involving 14 TFs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig6">6c</a>). We could infer that this <i>F3H</i> gene may be regulated in a hierarchical order by <i>WRKY Rhsim05G0226500</i> as the third regulator, either <i>GRAS Rhsim13G0080100</i> or <i>C3H Rhsim13G0068400</i> as the intermediate second regulator, and <i>MYB Rhsim08G0132300</i> or <i>C2H2 Rhsim10G0164300</i> as the direct regulator. When we incorporated the DNA binding site predictions, we found <i>C2H2</i> may be the sole intermediary regulator in the hierarchy, since only a C2H2 binding site was identified 5’ upstream of the <i>F3H</i> gene. By comprehensively examining the putative hierarchical gene regulation for all key enzymatic genes in anthocyanin/flavonol biosynthesis pathways (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig6">6c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">25</a>), <i>WRKY Rhsim05G0226500</i> was identified as an important upstream regulator in these pathways.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>We here describe a chromosome-scale genome assembly for a key parental species of the cultivated azalea, <i>Rhododendron simsii</i> (Ericales). In the asterids clade of the eudicot plants, representing ~25% of all flowering plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Soltis, D. E. et al. Polyploidy and angiosperm diversification. Am. J. Bot. 96, 336–348 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2869">27</a></sup>, we dated a whole-genome duplication in the Ericales lineage around 78 Mya. We believe this WGD event to be shared between Ericaceae and Actinidiaceae, as also suggested previously by Wei et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2873">20</a></sup>, while Soza et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Soza, V. L. et al. The Rhododendron genome and chromosomal organization provide insight into shared whole genome duplications across the heath family (Ericaceae). Genome Biol. Evol. 11, 3357–3371 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2877">7</a></sup> even suggested this WGD to have occurred in the common ancestor of Ericaceae, Actinidiaceae, and Theaceae. TD/PD, known to act as important drivers to increase gene product dosage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Conant, G. C. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Turning a hobby into a job: how duplicated genes find new functions. Nat. Rev. Genet. 9, 938–950 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2881">28</a></sup> and to accelerate the metabolic flux for rate limiting steps in certain biosynthetic pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Bekaert, M., Edger, P. P., Pires, J. C. &amp; Conant, G. C. Two-phase resolution of polyploidy in the Arabidopsis metabolic network gives rise to relative and absolute dosage constraints. Plant Cell 23, 1719–1728 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2886">29</a></sup>, were found to be highly enriched in certain gene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kliebenstein, D. J., Lambrix, V. M., Reichelt, M., Gershenzon, J. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Gene duplication in the diversification of secondary metabolism: tandem 2-oxoglutarate–dependent dioxygenases control glucosinolate biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 13, 681–693 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2890">30</a></sup>. We found that TD/PD duplications in azalea have substantially contributed to the proportions of enzymatic genes for the anthocyanin biosynthesis pathway, highly suggestive of the important role of TD/PD events in shaping flower color diversity.</p><p>Genetic modification in pigment biosynthetic pathways would provide a powerful method for obtaining novel flower colors beyond genetic constraints that are difficult to overcome through conventional breeding. Previous studies have characterized individual genes encoding relevant enzymes and gene transcription factors underlying pigment formation for floral crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tanaka, Y., Sasaki, N. &amp; Ohmiya, A. Biosynthesis of plant pigments: anthocyanins, betalains and carotenoids. Plant J. 54, 733–749 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2897">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun, T. et al. Carotenoid metabolism in plants: the role of plastids. Mol. Plant 11, 58–74 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2900">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Grotewold, E. The genetics and biochemistry of floral pigment. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 57, 761–780 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2903">32</a></sup>. Nevertheless, despite the studies on pigment composition for <i>Rhododendron</i> dating back to the 1950s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Riek, J. et al. Ornamental Crops. 237–271 (Springer, Cham, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2910">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="De Loose, R. The flower pigments of the Belgian hybrids of Rhododendron simsii and other species and varieties from Rhododendron subseries obtusum. Phytochemistry 8, 253–259 (1969)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2913">33</a></sup>, the molecular basis of azalea flower coloration has remained elusive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mizuta, D., Ban, T., Miyajima, I., Nakatsuka, A. &amp; Kobayashi, N. Comparison of flower color with anthocyanin composition patterns in evergreen azalea. Sci. Horticult. 122, 594–602 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2917">6</a></sup>, since, so far, only preliminary expression analysis was performed and for a limited number of structural genes.</p><p>Based on the chromosome-scale genome sequence of <i>R. simsii</i> presented here, in combination with the functional mapping and time-ordered transcriptome analysis, we first reconstructed the entire metabolic pathways for anthocyanin/flavonol/carotenoids biosynthesis in <i>Rhododendron</i>. The resolved pathways unravel that a MYB-bHLH-WD repeat (MBW) complex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ramsay, N. A. &amp; Glover, B. J. MYB-bHLH-WD40 protein complex and the evolution of cellular diversity. Trends Plant Sci. 10, 63–70 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2930">34</a></sup> composed of MYB, bHLH, and WD40 TFs collectively regulates flavonoids biosynthesis in flower tissues. Importantly, the time-ordered gene co-expression networks for the terminal stage of flower development show WRKY and ERF TFs at the center of the co-expression network, with all involved <i>WRKYs</i> belonging to Group II. We speculate that members of these two TF families, known to be responsible for biotic/abiotic stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Rushton, P. J., Somssich, I. E., Ringler, P. &amp; Shen, Q. J. WRKY transcription factors. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 247–258 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2937">35</a></sup>, may also be involved in flower pigment biosynthesis, and likely play vital roles in flower color intensity and patterning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Martins, T. R., Berg, J. J., Blinka, S., Rausher, M. D. &amp; Baum, D. A. Precise spatio-temporal regulation of the anthocyanin biosynthetic pathway leads to petal spot formation in Clarkia gracilis (Onagraceae). N. Phytol. 197, 958–969 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2942">36</a></sup>.</p><p>Flower colors of potted azalea are restricted to red, pink, white, reddish-purple, and purple, and providing yellow floral color has been the dream of many azalea breeders. Here, we reconstructed the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway known to be responsible for yellow, orange, or red pigments, as well as several genes encoding key enzymes of blue-anthocyanin biosynthesis responsible for blue pigment, none of which has been reported in <i>Rhododendron</i>. Moreover, gene expression for the generation of myricetin, kaempferol, and quercetin was detected as representatives of flavonols, which may also be involved in the flower color formation of <i>Rhododendron</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="De Loose, R. The flower pigments of the Belgian hybrids of Rhododendron simsii and other species and varieties from Rhododendron subseries obtusum. Phytochemistry 8, 253–259 (1969)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d64132e2954">33</a></sup>. These findings imply that potted azalea has the genetic potential to produce yellow and even extremely rare flower colors, such as violdelphin, and the genetic basis for flower coloring in <i>Rhododendron</i> far exceeds our cognition and it has great potential as a model plant for flower color research.</p><p>This study uncovered that MBW complexes may regulate <i>F3H</i> to control the biosynthesis of dihydroflavonols and subsequently also control anthocyanin/flavonol metabolism, suggesting that <i>F3H</i> should be a key node gene in regulating flower color formation in azalea. Moreover, the gene co-expression networks for metabolic pathways revealed that a specific WRKY transcription factor may play a core role in regulating flower coloration in azalea. We present a co-expression network for flower coloration, and inferred the potential contribution of individual members of transcription factor gene families, as well as structural genes involved in this regulatory process, a result that will allow for further functional investigation.</p><p>In conclusion, the reference genome sequence presented in this study provides a key resource for the further development of hypotheses and techniques in plant biotechnology, such as molecular marker-assisted breeding and genome editing, which are necessary to aid <i>Rhododendron</i> horticulture research and increase the efficiency for <i>Rhododendron</i> breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13"><i>Rhododendron simsii</i> plant material</h3><p>We sequenced the entire genome of a 20-year-old <i>Rhododendron simsii</i> individual originating from Jingshan, Hubei Province, China, and performed de novo genome assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">1</a>). This shrub had been transplanted to the Botanical Garden, Institute of Botany, Chinese Academy of Science, Beijing, China, and from which different plant tissues were sampled: fully expanded leaves were used for genome sequencing library preparation, while flowers, young leaves, and young stems were sampled for RNA sequencing (RNAseq) in support of genome annotation. Tissues were immediately flash frozen and stored at −80 °C for subsequent nucleic acid extractions.</p><p>In addition, corolla samples of five developmental stages from five individuals of <i>R. simsii</i> of the wild population selected for genome sequencing were collected to unravel the gene regulatory network underpinning flower coloring. Fresh tissues were first stored in RNAlater (Ambion, Life Technologies, Austin, TX, USA), then conserved at −80 °C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Library construction and sequencing of <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>Total DNA was isolated and extracted from the leaves using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (QIAGEN, Inc.) and then purified using the Mobio PowerClean Pro DNA Clean-Up Kit (MO BIO Laboratories, Inc.). For PacBio SMRT sequencing, sheared and concentrated DNA was used to construct sequencing libraries with 20-kb DNA inserts and subsequently run on a PacBio RSII platform using P6-C4 chemistry (6 SMRT cells). After data filtering and preprocessing, a total of 6.5 million PacBio long reads were generated, yielding ~50 Giga bases (Gb) (100× coverage) with an average read length of 7705 base pairs (bp).</p><p>For Illumina sequencing, 150-bp paired-end (PE) libraries were prepared for sequencing on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform. Short reads were processed with fastp (version 0.19.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Chen, S., Zhou, Y., Chen, Y. &amp; Gu, J. fastp: an ultra-fast all-in-one FASTQ preprocessor. Bioinformatics 34, i884–i890 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3022">37</a></sup>. Finally, we obtained ~91.49 Gb of raw sequencing data (corresponding to roughly 170× the assembled genome).</p><p>One Hi-C library prepared with MboI restriction enzyme was sequenced on Illumina HiSeq X Ten to generate ~55.68 Gb of valid data from 150 PE reads. Different tissues (stem, leaf, and flower) were used for constructing mRNA sequencing libraries; 150 bp PE sequencing was performed on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten machine.</p><p>More details on sample preparation and sequencing are available in the Supplementary Note 2 and 3.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">De novo genome assembly of <i>R. simsii</i> and quality control</h3><p>De novo genome assembly employed the three following steps: primary assembly, Hi-C scaffolding and polishing. First, eight versions of the primary assemblies were generated by SMARTdenovo (see “URLs” section), WTDBG (version 2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ruan, J. &amp; Li, H. Fast and accurate long-read assembly with wtdbg2. Nat. Meth. 17, 155–158 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3044">38</a></sup>, Canu (version 1.7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive K-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3048">39</a></sup> and FALCON-Phase (version 0.1.0-beta) (see “URLs” section) from PacBio long reads. Subsequently, we merged the two optimal assemblies, v0.3 (criteria: reasonably sized assembly, fewest contigs) and v0.4 (criterion: highest contig N50) by quickmerge (version 0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Chakraborty, M., Baldwin-Brown, J. G., Long, A. D. &amp; Emerson, J. J. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3052">40</a></sup>, further polished with one round of pilon (see “URLs” section) using clean Illumina reads to generate assembly v1.0. Based on Hi-C data and assembly v1.0, primary scaffolds were produced by 3D-DNA (version 180922) (see “URLs” section). These scaffolds were roughly spilt by Juicebox (version 1.8) (see “URLs” section) and another round of scaffolding by 3D-DNA (version 180922). Afterwards, we elaborately optimized the new scaffolds with gap closing by LR_Gapcloser (version 1.1) (see “URLs” section) and five rounds of pilon polishing.</p><p>BUSCO and LAI were used to assess genome completeness and continuity. Furthermore, we mapped 51.15 Gb PacBio sequencing reads, 91.49 Gb Illumina reads, 403.67 Gb clean RNA-seq sequences, and 55.68 raw Hi-C data to the genome assemble using BWA-MEM (see “URLs” section), minimap2 (version 2.11-r797)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094–3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3059">41</a></sup>, HiSat2 (version 2.1.0) (see “URLs” section) and Juicer (see “URLs” section), respectively.</p><p>Additional details regarding genome assembly and quality control are provided in the Supplementary Note 4.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Annotation of repeat elements and genes for <i>R. simsii</i>
</h3><p>Repeat elements were identified by a combination of homology-based and de novo approaches using RepeatModeler (version 1.0.10) (see “URLs” section) and RepeatMasker (version 4.0.7, rmblast-2.2.28) (see “URLs” section). Furthermore, we examined classification, age distribution, birth and death of LTR-RTs (17.01% of the annotated genome). In addition, amino acid sequences of intact RT domains of the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> superfamily were retrieved to perform selection pressure analyses with codeml<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3084">42</a></sup>.</p><p>De novo and reference genome-guided transcriptome assemblies were constructed and combined as evidence for coding gene prediction. Coding gene structures were predicted by MAKER2 pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinf. 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3091">43</a></sup> applied to the repeat-masked genome with three main approaches (ab initio predictions, homolog proteins and transcriptome data). Non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) were annotated using several databases and software packages: tRNAscan-SE (version 1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3095">44</a></sup>, RNAmmer (version 1.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3100–3108 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3099">45</a></sup>, Rfam database (version 9.1) (see “URLs” section), and BLASTn (version 2.2.28+). Gene functions were annotated according to both homology and similarity searches by blat (version 36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3103">46</a></sup> with 30% identity and 1e−05 <i>E</i>-value cutoff and domain similarity prediction strategies using InterProScan (version 5.27-66.0) (see “URLs” section).</p><p>Locations of all centromeres within the genome were inferred with Centurion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Varoquaux, N. et al. Accurate identification of centromere locations in yeast genomes using Hi-C. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, 5331–5339 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3113">47</a></sup> based on corrected Hi-C data and the tendency of formed clusters in three-dimensional space.</p><p>Additional details are available in the Supplementary Note 5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Inference and analysis of orthogroups for asterids</h3><p>To retrieve the evolutionary history of the asterids clade, a total of 22,455 orthogroups of azalea and 16 representative plant species (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">13</a>) were identified by using OrthoFinder (version 2.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3135">22</a></sup>. Then, OrthoFinder provides a formalized procedure for determining 806 low-copy orthologs with minimum of 76.5% of species having single-copy genes in any orthogroup. The concatenated amino acid sequences alignment was created by MUSCLE (version 3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3139">48</a></sup> and trimmed with trimAI (version 1.2) (trimal -gt 0.8 -st 0.001 -cons 60)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Capella-Gutiérrez, S., Silla-Martínez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldón, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3143">49</a></sup>. And then a maximum likelihood (ML) phylogenetic tree was constructed using IQ-TREE (version 1.6.7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3148">50</a></sup> with the selected optimal sequence evolution model (-m JTT + F + R5) and with ultrafast bootstrapping (-bb 1000)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Minh, B. Q., Nguyen, M. A. &amp; von Haeseler, A. Ultrafast approximation for phylogenetic bootstrap. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1188–1195 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3152">51</a></sup> using <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> as outgroups. The ML tree was then used as an input tree to estimate the divergence time using the MCMCTree program in the PAML package (version 4.9 h)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3162">42</a></sup> with two fossils and a soft bound at three nodes: (1) the stem node of <i>Rhododendron</i> (56 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Collinson, M. E. &amp; Crane, P. R. Rhododendron seeds from the Palaeocene of southern England. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 76, 195–205 (1978)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3170">52</a></sup>, (2) the crown node of Ericales (89.8 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Nixon, K. C. &amp; Crepet, W. L. Late Cretaceous fossil flowers of Ericalean affinity. Am. J. Bot. 80, 616–623 (1993)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3174">53</a></sup>, (3) asterids-rosids (116–126 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3178">54</a></sup> as constraints for calibrating the age of our tree. The expansion and contraction of gene families were inferred with CAFÉ (version 4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3182">23</a></sup> based on the chronogram of these 17 species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome duplication and intergenomic comparison for asterids</h3><p>To investigate genome evolution across asterids, we searched for genome-wide duplications in the order Cornales and Ericales. First, we identified different modes of gene duplication as whole-genome duplicates (WGD), tandem duplicates (TD), proximal duplicates (less than 10 gene distance on the same chromosome: PD), transposed duplicates (transposed gene duplications: TRD), or dispersed duplicates (other duplicates than WGD, TD, PD and TRD: DSD) using DupGen_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Qiao, X. et al. Gene duplication and evolution in recurring polyploidization-diploidization cycles in plants. Genome Biol. 20, 38 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3194">55</a></sup> with default parameters. Second, the <i>K</i>a (number of substitutions per nonsynonymous site), <i>K</i>s (number of substitutions per synonymous site), and <i>K</i>a/<i>K</i>s values were estimated for gene pairs generated by different modes of duplication based on the YN model in KaKs_Calculator (version 2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genomics Proteom. Bioinform. 8, 77–80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3211">56</a></sup>, after conversion of amino acid alignments into the corresponding codon alignments with PAL2NAL (version 14)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3215">57</a></sup>.</p><p>For intergenomic comparison, we compared <i>Vitis vinifera</i> genome with five asterids species (<i>Camellia sinensis</i>, <i>Camptotheca acuminata</i>, <i>Rhododendron delavayi</i>, <i>R. williamsianum</i>, <i>R. simsii</i>), and also compared <i>R. simsii</i> genome with that of <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> and a haplotype of <i>Vaccinium corymbosum</i>. The orthologs between these species were identified using MCScanX_h<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3250">58</a></sup>. Subsequently, <i>Ks</i> substitution rates of gene pairs across syntenic blocks were calculated. Finally, we illustrated <i>Ks</i> distribution and the dotplots of orthologous blocks using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3261">58</a></sup>.</p><p>WGD time was estimated with asterids-rosids divergence time (mean: 121 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3268">54</a></sup> as an age constraint. <i>K</i>s peaks of <i>V. vinifera</i> vs. five species (<i>Camellia sinensis</i>, <i>Camptotheca acuminata</i>, <i>R. delavayi</i>, <i>R. williamsianum</i>, and <i>R. simsii</i>) syntenic orthologs allows calculating <i>Ks</i> per year (<i>r</i>) following <i>r</i> = <i>K</i>s/(2 × (divergence time))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3307">16</a></sup>. We applied the same <i>r</i> value to calculate the time of WGD events for each species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Flowering time and flower color genes</h3><p>We searched for homologs of flowering time control genes in <i>A. thaliana</i> by querying FLOR-ID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bouche, F., Lobet, G., Tocquin, P. &amp; Perilleux, C. FLOR-ID: an interactive database of flowering-time gene networks in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1167–D1171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3325">24</a></sup>, a recently developed database which categorizes 295 protein-coding and 11 miRNA genes and describes their interactions within Arabidopsis’ genome. Flower color is mainly determined by anthocyanins and carotenoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tanaka, Y., Sasaki, N. &amp; Ohmiya, A. Biosynthesis of plant pigments: anthocyanins, betalains and carotenoids. Plant J. 54, 733–749 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3329">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wessinger, C. A. &amp; Rausher, M. D. Lessons from flower colour evolution on targets of selection. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 5741–5749 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3332">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Grotewold, E. The genetics and biochemistry of floral pigment. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 57, 761–780 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3335">32</a></sup>. Given the importance of anthocyanins/carotenoids accumulation in <i>R. simsii</i>, we annotated genes associated with the anthocyanins/carotenoids biosynthesis pathways by querying the Plant Metabolic Network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Schlapfer, P. et al. Genome-wide prediction of metabolic enzymes, pathways, and gene clusters in plants. Plant Physiol. 173, 2041–2059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3342">59</a></sup> and validated by Semi-Automated Validation Infrastructure (version 3.02)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Schlapfer, P. et al. Genome-wide prediction of metabolic enzymes, pathways, and gene clusters in plants. Plant Physiol. 173, 2041–2059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3347">59</a></sup>, after enzymatic protein annotations for coding genes through the Ensemble Enzyme Prediction Pipeline (E2P2) package (version 3.1) (see “URLs” section).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20"><i>R. simsii</i> transcription factors</h3><p>We used PlantRegMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tian, F., Yang, D. C., Meng, Y. Q., Jin, J. &amp; Gao, G. PlantRegMap: charting functional regulatory maps in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 48, D1104–D1113 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3362">60</a></sup> to identify TFs in the <i>R. simsii</i> genome. Among all identified transcription factors, we further analyzed the phylogeny, gene conserved motifs, and protein structures of MYB, bHLH, WD40, WRKY and ERF. Additional details are available in the Supplementary Note 6 and 7.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Metabolic pathways construction based on TO-GCNs</h3><p>In order to identify core genes of flower timing and color development, we performed a gene expression study using full transcriptome data. For the gene expression study, 25 frozen corolla tissues from five developmental stages and five-times replicates were ground with a mortar and pestle and RNA was isolated using the NEBNext Poly (A) mRNA Magnetic Isolation Module. RNA quality was determined on an Agilent 2100 BioAnalyzer. All of the 25 sequencing libraries were prepared using the NEBNext Ultra RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina. Next, 150 bp PE mRNA sequencing was performed on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten. These Illumina reads were processed using Trimmomatic (version 0.36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3377">61</a></sup> and Cutadapt (version 1.13)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Martin, M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. EMBnet J. 17, 10–12 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3381">62</a></sup>, and then mapped to the final assembly using HiSat2 (version 2.1.0). Only uniquely mapped paired-end reads were retained for read counting of the annotated genes by featureCounts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Liao, Y., Smyth, G. K. &amp; Shi, W. featureCounts: an efficient general purpose program for assigning sequence reads to genomic features. Bioinformatics 30, 923–930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3385">63</a></sup> to generate the count and Transcripts per Kilobase Million (TPM) tables. Differential gene expression (DEG) analyses between the five stages were performed with DEseq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3389">64</a></sup>, with 0.05 as the FDR cut-off and a log2 fold change (FC) cut-off of 1 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#MOESM1">20</a>).</p><p>We used a recently developed method for reconstructing time-ordered gene co-expression networks (TO-GCNs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chang, Y. M. et al. Comparative transcriptomics method to infer gene coexpression networks and its applications to maize and rice leaf transcriptomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 3091–3099 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3399">26</a></sup>). Significantly differentially expressed genes with average TPM less than 0.5 were excluded. The Pearson correlations between 618 TFs and 7,449 genes less than −0.57 or above 0.81 in C1 + C2 + GCN were visualized in graphs using Cytoscape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Shannon, P. et al. Cytoscape: a software environment for integrated models of biomolecular interaction networks. Genome Res. 13, 2498–2504 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3403">65</a></sup>.</p><p>Finally, 2 Kb sequences in the upstream of six core genes (<i>Rhsim03G0111400, Rhsim08G0132300, Rhsim10G0164300, Rhsim11G0126300, Rhsim13G0068400</i>, and <i>Rhsim13G0080100</i>) were extracted and queried against PlantRegMap and PlantCARE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Lescot, M. et al. PlantCARE, a database of plant cis-acting regulatory elements and a portal to tools for in silico analysis of promoter sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 325–327 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3416">66</a></sup>. TF binding sites uncovered by PlantRegMap were illustrated with R package drawProteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Brennan, P. drawProteins: a Bioconductor/R package for reproducible and programmatic generation of protein schematics. F1000Research 7, 1105 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3420">67</a></sup> (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig5">5</a>d and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig6">6b</a>). Additional details regarding TFs are provided in the Supplementary Note 8.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Gene functional enrichment</h3><p>Hypergeometric tests were performed to determine whether specific functional categories from GO were significantly overrepresented in <i>R. simsii</i> gene sets within the genome. Functional enrichment was tested using the R package clusterProfiler (version 3.6.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Yu, G., Wang, L. G., Han, Y. &amp; He, Q. Y. clusterProfiler: an R package for comparing biological themes among gene clusters. OMICS J. Integr. Biol. 16, 284–287 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d64132e3442">68</a></sup> with the background set of all annotated <i>R. simsii</i> genes in this study.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">URLs</h3><p>SMARTdenovo [<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo">https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo</a>];                 <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during this study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The raw sequence data of <i>R. simsii</i> genome sequencing and RNA sequencing have been deposited in NCBI under the accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP229032">SRP229032</a> (Bio-Project <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA588298">PRJNA588298</a>). The final assembly is available at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/WJXA00000000">WJXA00000000</a>. Genome assembly, repeat and gene annotation, gene expression profiles could be downloaded and explored online under URL <a href="http://rhododendron.plantgenie.org/">http://rhododendron.plantgenie.org/</a>. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig1">1</a>a, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18771-4#Fig3">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor"
